[1] Chapter 1
[2] Colin Lamb's Narrative
[3] 1
[4] According to police records: At 2:59 PM on September 9th, I was walking west on Wilbraham Crescent. This was my first time here, and I must admit, everything about the place left me bewildered.
[5] For some time now, I've had a feeling, and this feeling has been growing stronger and stronger, almost impossible to shake off. That's just how I felt.
[6] I was looking for a house numbered 61, and I thought, maybe I won't find it. I had carefully found houses from 1 to 35, and just then, I discovered that Wilbraham Crescent had reached its end. A road called Albany Avenue rudely blocked my path. I turned around. There were no houses to the north, only a wall. Behind this wall, there were towering modern apartments, and it was clear that the entrance was on another road. It was impossible to find a way from here.
[7] As I walked, I paid attention to the house numbers I passed by – 24, 23, 22, 21, Diana's Cottage (which must be number 20, with an orange cat washing itself on the gatepost), and 19.
[8] The door to number 19 suddenly opened, and a young girl rushed out, then ran up the path. I thought a missile's speed was nothing compared to hers. She ran with such a sharp cry that she looked even more like a missile. The sound was high-pitched and pitiful. The girl ran through the gate and collided with me, almost knocking me off the sidewalk. She not only collided with me but also grabbed me tightly, desperately clinging to me.
[9] "Take it easy," I said, gently shaking her when I regained my balance, "Now take it easy."
[10] The girl's emotions finally calmed down. Although she was still holding on to me tightly, she was no longer screaming, but only panting heavily, gasping and sobbing.
[11] In this situation, I must say that my reaction was not quick enough. I asked her what happened. Realizing that my tone was too weak, I immediately changed it.
[12] "What happened?"
[13] The girl took a deep breath.
[14] "Over there!" She pointed to the place behind her.
[15] "Then what?"
[16] "There was a man lying on the floor... dead... she was about to step on him."
[17] "Who? How did this happen?"
[18] "I think, because she's blind. And there was blood on him."
[19] "Indeed, there was blood." I said, looking at the stain on my sleeve. "Now I'm the same." I pointed to the stain. Thinking about the current situation, I sighed. "You should take me there to see."
[20] But she began to shake her head vigorously.
[21] "I can't go, I can't go... I don't want to go back there."
[22] "Maybe you're right." I looked around. There didn't seem to be a place to put a girl who was about to faint. I gently guided her, letting her lean against the iron railing.
[23] "Stay here," I said, "don't leave until I come back. I won't be long." I leaned forward and if you feel uncomfortable, put your head between your knees, it will feel better."
[24] "I, I think I'm okay now."
[25] Although she said so, she was still a little shaken, but I didn't want to talk to her too much. I gently patted her shoulder, comforted her, and then walked quickly up the path. I went through the door into the house, hesitated slightly in the hallway, observed the left door, which was an empty restaurant, and then walked through the foyer into the opposite living room.
[26] The first thing I saw was an old woman with gray hair sitting on a chair. When I went in, her head turned quickly towards me and said,
[27] "Who came?"
[28] I immediately realized that the woman's eyes were blind. Her eyes looked straight at me, then swept past me and landed on the back of my left ear.
[29] I said directly:
[30] "A young girl ran up the street, saying that someone died here."
[31] I felt that what I said was absurd. In this clean room, the woman sat with her hands folded on the chair, looking so calm. It seemed impossible to find a dead man here.
[32] But she immediately answered.
[33] "Behind the sofa," she said.
[34] I went around the corner of the sofa. Then I saw the open arms, the vacant eyes, and the congealed blood clots.
[35] "How did this happen?" I suddenly asked.
[36] "I don't know."
[37] "So, who is he?"
[38] "I don't know."
[39] "We have to call the police." I look around. "Where's the phone?" "I don't have a phone."
[40] I stare at her intently.
[41] "Are you living here? Is this your house?"
[42] "Yes."
[43] "Can you tell me what happened?"
[44] "Of course. I came back with my shopping -" I noticed the shopping bag thrown on the chair near the door. "I came home and immediately sensed that there was someone else in the house. A blind person is very sensitive to such things. I asked who was in the house. No one answered me. I only heard someone breathing heavily. I went towards the source of the sound. Then someone yelled out - someone had died, I was about to step on the body. Then someone screamed past me, running out of the room."
[45] I nodded. They both told the same story.
[46] "What did you do then?"
[47] "I walked carefully until my foot touched something."
[48] "What happened next?"
[49] "I squatted down. I felt a man's hand. It was cold and there was no pulse… I got up and walked over here, sat down and waited for someone to come. It won't be long, someone will come. That young woman, whoever she is, will call for help. I think it's best not to leave this house."
[50] I was shocked by the woman's calmness. She didn't scream or panic in the house, she just sat there quietly waiting. She was rational, but we still had to take action.
[51] She questioned:
[52] "Who are you?"
[53] "My name is Colin Lamb. I just happened to pass by."
[54] "Where did the young girl go?"
[55] "I let her rest near the entrance. She was scared. Where is the nearest place to call for help?"
[56] "Follow this path for about fifty yards, and there's a phone booth just before the corner."
[57] "Yes, I remember passing by there. I'm going to call the police. You—" I hesitated.
[58] I didn't know whether to say "Are you going to stay here?" or "Are you okay?"
[59] She spared me the decision.
[60] "You should bring the girl inside," she said firmly.
[61] "I don't know if she'll come," I said skeptically.
[62] "Of course, don't let her come into this room. Tell her to wait in the dining room next to the entrance. Tell her I'm brewing tea for her."
[63] She approached me.
[64] "But can you do that?" she asked.
[65] A slight smirk appeared on her face.
[66] "My dear young man, since I moved into this house fourteen years ago, I have been cooking in the kitchen myself. Being blind doesn't mean I'm useless."
[67] "I'm sorry, I was too ignorant. Perhaps I can ask for your name?"
[68] "Millee Pema-Chhoe – Miss."
[69] I left the room and walked out along the path. The girl saw me and struggled to get up.
[70] "I, I think I'm almost better now."
[71] I helped her stand up, smiling,
[72] "That's good."
[73] "There, there, someone died, right?"
[74] I immediately nodded.
[75] "Indeed. I'm going to the phone booth to report the incident to the police. If I were you, I would wait in the house. Go to the restaurant and wait on your left as you enter. Miss Millechamp is brewing tea for you." I raised my voice to prevent her from immediately contradicting me. "Wait in the restaurant, on your left as you enter. Miss Millechamp is brewing tea for you."
[76] "So that's Miss Pemakh, is it? Can she see?"
[77] "Yes, she is. This has certainly shocked her, but she has been very rational about it. Come on, I'll take you in. Having a cup of tea while waiting for the police will make you feel better."
[78] I put my arm around her shoulder and led her down the path. After settling her comfortably at the table, I rushed to the phone booth.

[80] A cold voice said, "Croedin Police Station."
[81] "Is Inspector Hatchet there?"
[82] The voice suddenly became cautious:
[83] "I don't know. Who is this?"
[84] "Tell him it's Colin Loom."

[87] "Is that Colin? It's been a while. Where are you?"
[88] "Crofton. To be precise, I'm on the floor of 19 Wilbraham Crescent. There's a man lying there, dead, I think stabbed. Probably died about half an hour ago."
[89] "Who found him? You?"
[90] "No, a stranger. A girl came running out of the house, almost knocking me over. She said there was a man lying on the floor of the house, dead. A blind woman almost stepped on his body."
[91] "You're not pulling my leg, are you?" Dick asked skeptically.
[92] "I admit, it sounds incredible, but that's the truth. The woman is the owner of the house, Miss Mirella Pembleton."
[93] "The one who almost tripped over the dead man?"
[94] "Not like you think. If she was blind, she wouldn't have noticed the dead man lying there."
[95] "I'll be right there. Wait for me there. How did you take care of the girl?"
[96] "Miss Pemah is brewing tea for her."
[97] Dic commented that the arrangement sounded somewhat soothing.
[98] Chapter Two
[99] At 19 Wilbraham Crescent, the police officers had arrived. A forensic expert, a photographer, and a fingerprint examiner were present. They worked in an orderly manner, with each person performing their designated task efficiently.
[100] The last to arrive was Inspector Hakast, a tall man with expressive eyebrows and a stern expression. He observed that all the work he had arranged was proceeding smoothly. He glanced at the corpse, exchanged a few brief words with the forensic expert, and then went to the dining room. There, he found three people seated with three empty teacups in front of them. They were Miss Pemah, Colin Lum, and a tall girl with brown curls. The girl's eyes were wide with fear. "Very nice," the inspector remarked in his usual witty manner.
[101] He introduced himself to Miss Pema.

[103] He knew something about Miss Pema, despite their lines of work never intersecting. He had seen her a few times before, knowing her to be a teacher at the Allenburg Institute, teaching blind script to disabled children. The discovery of a man dead of murder in such a clean, simple house seemed improbable. But often the most improbable things are the most common.
[104] "This is a truly awful thing, Miss Pema," he said, "and I'm afraid it's quite a shock to you. I need to get a clear understanding of the whole incident from you. I know -" he glanced quickly at the police report he had been given, "Hilary Webb was the one who actually found the body. Miss Pema, if you allow me to use your kitchen, I would like to bring Miss Webb there and speak with her privately."

[1352] Chapter 13
[1353] Colin Lamb's Narrative
[1354] I walked down Charing Cross Road, turning into a maze of streets between New Oxford Street and Covent Garden. There were all sorts of shops you wouldn't expect: antique shops, doll hospitals, ballet shoe shops, and foreign delicatessens.
[1355] I resisted the temptation of the various blue or brown glass eyes in the doll hospital and finally arrived at my destination. It was a dimly lit street-side bookstore near the British Museum. There were usually books displayed on the outside tray, ranging from classic novels to old textbooks and all sorts of miscellaneous books, each with a price tag. Some of the books were almost complete in pages, and occasionally there were pristine, unblemished ones.
[1356] I squeezed through the doorway. With more and more books piled up on both sides of the entrance, there was a constant risk of collapse, so I had to squeeze through sideways. Once inside, you would find that the books occupied all the space. There were books everywhere, lying flat or standing upright, in all sorts of positions, obviously ready to topple over at the slightest touch. The distance between the shelves was so narrow that it took a lot of effort to walk through them. These piles of books were temporarily placed on shelves or tables. A chair in the corner was surrounded by books, and an old man sat on it, wearing a flat-crowned, rolled-brim hat, with a flat face like a stuffed fish. He looked weary, like someone who had given up fighting for equality. He tried to control the books, but it was clear that the books had controlled him. He was King Canute in the world of books, who chose to withdraw before the tide of books swept over him. This was Mr. Solomon, the bookstore owner. He recognized me, and his cold gaze softened slightly, nodding at me.
[1357] "Do you have any books I want?" I asked.
[1358] "You'll have to go up and look for yourself, Mr. Lamb. Are you still researching seaweed or something similar?"
[1359] "Yes."
[1360] "Oh, then you know where to find it. Ocean biology, fossils, Antarctica, on the third floor. I received a new package yesterday. Although it's opened, it hasn't been sorted yet. It's in the corner over there. Take a look yourself."
[1361] I nodded and walked sideways, making my way to the back of the store, where a dirty, unstable staircase led to the second floor. Here, there were books on Eastern history, art, medicine, and French classical literature. Every corner of this floor was draped with curtains. This was an intriguing corner unknown to the general public but open to experts and scholars. It was the exclusive storage area for books referred to as "treasures." I walked over and continued up to the third floor.
[1362] Here, various archaeological, zoological, and other highly regarded volumes were casually categorized. I dodged students, elderly army colonels, and priests, turning the corner of the bookshelf, stepping over many opened packages lying on the floor, filled with books, when I found that my path was blocked by a couple of lovers, lost in their own world, swaying back and forth.
[1363] "Sorry." I pushed them aside with force, lifted the curtain covering the door, deftly took the key from my pocket, turned the lock, and walked in. I found myself awkwardly standing in the doorway. The walls were freshly painted, and a framed print of a yak hung on them. A shiny door handle glinted on the door. I carefully tapped the door handle, and the door opened. A middle-aged woman with gray hair emerged, wearing an old-fashioned, oddly shaped pair of glasses, dressed in a black skirt, and surprisingly paired with a mint green wicker knit sweater.
[1364] "Is it you?" She didn't greet me, getting straight to the point. "He asked about you yesterday. He was not happy." She shook her head at me, as if an older family tutor shaking her head at a disappointing child. "You could try to do better," she said.
[1365] "Oh, spare me, Nanny," I said.
[1366] "Don't call me Nanny," the middle-aged woman said. "It's rude. I've told you that before."
[1367] "It's your fault," I said. "You shouldn't talk to me like a child."
[1368] "You should grow up," she said. "Come in and get to work."
[1369] She pressed the bell, picked up the telephone receiver from the table, and said:

[1454] Chapter 14
[1455] Narrative by Colin Lamb
[1456] I haven't been to Whitehall House for a long time. A few years ago, it was a grand modern apartment building. Now, there are many impressive and more modern-looking buildings on both sides of the street. Going inside, I found that the building has recently been renovated. The walls have been repainted in light yellow or light green.
[1457] I took the elevator upstairs and rang the doorbell at room 203. The door was opened by a polite male servant named George. He greeted me with a welcoming smile.
[1458] "Mr. Colin! It's been a while since I've seen you."
[1459] "Yes, you're doing well, George?"
[1460] "I'm doing well, thank you, sir."
[1461] I lowered my voice. "How is he?"
[1462] George also lowered his voice, although it seemed unnecessary, as he had been speaking carefully from the beginning.

[1464] I sympathetically nodded.
[1465] "This way, sir -" He took my hat.

[1467] "Certainly, sir." He opened the door and called out in a clear voice. "Mr. Colin Lamb to see you, sir."
[1468] He stepped back, allowing me to pass, and I entered the room.
[1469] My friend, Hercule Poirot, sat in his usual large, comfortable armchair by the fireplace. I noticed a long, rectangular electric fire glowing red. It was still warm in early September, but Poirot, ever the first to sense the coming chill of autumn, had prepared accordingly. Books were neatly stacked on the floor to either side of him, and more on the desk to his left. A steaming cup of tea sat on the table to his right. I guessed it was some kind of herbal concoction. He was fond of such things and often insisted on sharing them with me. They tasted and smelled unpleasant.
[1470] "Don't get up," I said. But Poirot had already risen. He spread his arms and came towards me, his polished shoes gleaming.
[1471] "Ah, it is you, my friend! My young friend, Colin. But why do you call yourself Lambe? Let me think. There is a saying or proverb. It's something like 'an old sheep in sheep's clothing' – a metaphor for an elderly woman trying to dress like a young, pretty one. It doesn't suit you. Ah, I have it. You are a wolf in sheep's clothing, are you not?"
[1472] "No, not at all," I said. "It's just that using my real name in my work is not advisable, as it would involve my father to some extent. So I go by Lambe. It's short and easy to remember, and it suits me. I may be a bit self-aggrandizing, but please don't mind."
[1473] "Is that so?" Poirot said. "How is your father, my dear friend?"
[1474] "He's doing well," I said. "He's always busy with his roses or chrysanthemums or whatever flowers are in season. Time flies, and I can't remember what bloomed at that time."
[1475] "So he's been busy with his gardening?"
[1476] "Everyone seems to do that as they age," I said.
[1477] "Not me," said Hercule Poirot. "I used to love growing zucchinis, yes, but that's all in the past. If you want the best flowers, why not go to the florist? I think that good detective is going to write his memoirs?"
[1478] "He has started," I said. "But he found that there was a lot that needed to be deleted, and in the end, he concluded that what remained was too dull to be worth writing down."
[1479] "It's a habit that comes with the job, yes," said Poirot. "It's unfortunate. Because your father has some very interesting stories. I admire him. You know, his methods are very interesting to me. He's always so straightforward. He uses methods that no one has ever used before. He sets a trap, a very obvious trap, so that the person he wants to catch often says, 'This is too obvious. It can't be true.' And then they fall into the trap!"
[1480] I laughed. "Yes," I said. "It's not fashionable these days for sons to admire their fathers. They seem to sit down and write their memoirs, filled with bitterness and resentment, remembering all the unpleasant things they can remember. But personally, I respect my father. I even hope to be as good as him, of course not by following his every step."
[1481] "But it's almost the same," Poirot said. "Almost exactly the same, although you have to work behind the scenes, and he doesn't. He coughed lightly. "I should congratulate you on your recent remarkable success. The Larkin case, isn't it?"
[1482] "Things are going well so far," I said, "but I have much more to do. And I didn't come here today to talk about that."
[1483] "Of course, of course," Poirot said, waving for me to sit in a chair and offering me a cup of herbal tea, which I declined.

[1485] "What have you been up to lately?" I asked Poirot.
[1486] "Looking at all these books around you," I said, "it seems you've been doing some research?"
[1487] Poirot sighed. "You could say that. Yes, in a way, it's true. Recently, I've been eager to find a problem. Any problem will do, I tell myself, like Sherlock Holmes. The depth of parsley in butter. The most important thing is to have a problem. I need to exercise not muscles, you understand, but brain cells."
[1488] "Just staying healthy," I said.
[1489] "As you said," he sighed. "But, dear, this problem is hard to come by. Last Thursday, someone brought me such a problem. Three dry orange peels appeared on my umbrella stand for no reason. [1633] Chapter 15
[1634] Many people attended the trial. Due to the panic caused by the murder case, the people of Crofton showed great enthusiasm for the revelation of the sensational event. However, the trial process was not as interesting as imagined. Miss Weaver had no need to fear the severe examination, as it lasted only a few minutes.
[1635] A phone call came to the Cavendish Typewriting Agency, directly asking for her to go to 19 Wilbraham Crescent. She arrived there and, as instructed, entered the living room. There she found the deceased and screamed, fleeing the house for help. There were no questions or requests for further detailed explanations. At the same time, Miss Martin provided evidence, and her questioning was shorter. She received a phone call claiming to be from Miss Pemukh, asking to arrange a stenographer to go to 19 Wilbraham Crescent, specifying Miss Weaver, and clearly instructing what to do upon entering the house. She recorded the time the call came in as 1:49 PM. Thus, Miss Martin also passed the test.
[1636] Miss Pembroke denied the above statements. She did not call Cavendish Typewriting Agency to request any typist to go to her. Inspector Hatch made a brief, routine statement. He went to 19 Wilbraham Crescent as soon as he received the phone call and found the body of the deceased.
[1637] "Have you identified the victim?" asked the coroner.
[1638] "No, sir. For this reason, I request a postponement of the trial."
[1639] "Indeed, it should be so."
[1640] Next, medical evidence was presented. Dr. Riggs, representing the police, briefly introduced his work and professional qualifications, recounted his journey to 19 Wilbraham Crescent, and described his examination of the deceased.
[1641] "Can you estimate the approximate time of death, doctor?"
[1642] "I examined him at 3:30. I estimate the time of death to be between 1:30 and 2:30."
[1643] "Can you be more precise?"
[1644] "I'm afraid not. It is most likely to be around 2:00 or earlier, but there are many factors to consider, such as age and health status."
[1645] "Have you examined the body?"
[1646] "Yes."
[1647] "What was the cause of death?"
[1648] "The victim was stabbed to death with a thin and sharp knife, possibly a French cooking knife with a tapering blade. The tip of the blade went in... the doctor skillfully described the precise location of the knife entering the heart."
[1649] "Was the victim instantly killed?"
[1650] "It was probably within a few minutes."
[1651] "Did the victim cry out or struggle?"
[1652] "Based on the way he was stabbed, it appears he did not."
[1653] "Can you explain that to us, doctor?"
[1654] "I examined his internal organs and conducted some experiments. What I mean to say is, when he was stabbed, he was in a coma, caused by the effects of a drug."
[1655] "Can you tell us what kind of drug that was, doctor?"
[1656] "Certainly. It's called chloral hydrate."
[1657] "Can you explain how it works?"
[1658] "I can only speculate that it was mixed with something like alcohol and consumed. Chloral hydrate has a very fast onset of action."
[1659] "Like adding an anesthetic to the drink, I suppose," the coroner murmured.
[1660] "Exactly," said Dr. Riggs, "he drank this liquid without any suspicion, and soon felt dizzy before losing consciousness."
[1661] "So, in your opinion, he was killed while unconscious?"
[1662] "That's what I think. It also explains why there were no signs of struggle and why he looked peaceful when he died."
[1663] "How long after losing consciousness did he die?"
[1664] "I can't give you an exact time. It depends on the victim's individual physiology. But he definitely wouldn't have woken up within half an hour, and it's likely he took longer than that."
[1665] "Thank you, Dr. Rig. Do you have any evidence of the time the victim last ate?"
[1666] "If that's what you mean, he didn't have lunch. He hadn't eaten solid food for at least four hours."
[1667] "Thank you, Dr. Rig. I think I have no more questions."

[1670] The trial ended, and people began to move towards the courtroom exit. Irena Brent and the other girls from the Cavenish Typewriting Agency were together. [1685] She almost blurted out:

[1687] Suddenly, resolute, she turned off the main street and onto Albany Road, leading to Wilbraham Crescent.
[1688] Ever since the media reported a murder at 19 Wilbraham Crescent, there had been a steady stream of people gathering in front of the house to get a glimpse of the scene. In this situation, even the bricks and mortar of the house became a tangible source of intrigue for the onlookers. For twenty-four hours after the incident, a police officer stood guard to maintain order among the pedestrians. Though interest waned, it did not completely dissipate. Delivery trucks slowed down as they passed by, and pushchairs with infants would pause for four or five minutes on the opposite sidewalk, staring intently at Miss Pemaroo's well-kept residence, their minds wandering with thoughts. Shopping women with baskets paused, chatting happily with friends.

[1697] "The owner of this house is blind. Poor thing. So, of course, she wouldn't know what happened."
[1698] "Oh, but she wasn't there when it happened…"
[1699] "Oh, I thought she was, I thought she was upstairs, and I heard his voice. Oh, dear, I must go shopping."
[1700] Such similar conversations can be heard all the time. As if drawn by a magnet, all sorts of people come to Wilbraham Crescent, stop, look, and then move on. Some desires are too strong to resist."
[1701] Here, Ina Brent is still puzzled. She finds herself being pushed along, caught between five or six people, all absorbed in the pleasure of observing the haunted house.
[1702] Ina is easily influenced, and she begins to look around, too.
[1703] So this is the house where the murder happened! The windows are hung with net curtains. It looks so cozy. Yet, a man was killed there, with a common kitchen knife, almost every kitchen has one…
[1704] As if hypnotized by the behavior of the people around her, Ina stares, stopping her thoughts…
[1705] She almost forgets why she came here…
[1706] When a voice rings in her ear, she jumps in surprise.
[1707] She turns her head and looks at the source of the voice with a shocked expression.

[2142] Inspector Hercule Hachette looked at the calendar on the table. September 20th. Ten days had passed. The case had not progressed as expected, and the same problem remained in front of them: the identity of the deceased was still unconfirmed. More time had been spent than anticipated. The leads collected were gradually reduced to nothing after investigation. The lab results on the clothes did not reveal anything special. The clothes themselves did not provide any clues. They were of high quality, an export item, not very new but well preserved. The dentist's office had nothing to report; the laundry shop, the janitor, none of them had anything. The deceased remained a "mysterious figure," but Inspector Hachette believed he was not a "mysterious figure." He had nothing remarkable about him. He was just someone who had not been identified yet. That was the problem, he was sure. As he thought of the photo—with the prominent headline "Do you know this man?" published in the newspaper—the flood of phone calls and letters that followed, Inspector Hachette sighed. So many people thought they recognized the man in the photo. Daughters wrote letters to find their long-lost fathers. An elderly woman of about ninety was convinced that the man in the photo was her son who had run away thirty years ago. There were countless wives who believed that the man in the photo was their missing husband. The sisters did not rush to confirm that he was their brother, perhaps because they were not too hopeful. Of course, there was a large group of people who claimed to have seen the man in Lincolnshire, Newcastle, Devon, and London, or on the subway, on the bus, hiding in a dock, or lurking at a street corner, or in a dark corner of a movie theater, trying not to be noticed. There were hundreds of leads, all of which seemed to offer hope of discovering something, but ultimately yielded nothing.
[2143] But today, the inspector felt a glimmer of hope. He looked at the letters on the table again. Melina Rival. He didn't like the nickname. In his opinion, rational people would not choose the name Melina for their child's baptism. Without a doubt, it was the lady who chose the name herself. But he liked the feeling of the letter. It was neither too extravagant nor too self-assured. The letter simply stated that she felt the man in the photo was her long-separated husband. She would be visiting him this morning. He rang the bell, and Inspector Grey entered.
[2144] "Mrs. Rival hasn't arrived yet?"
[2145] "Just arrived," said Grey. "I was just about to tell you."

She was not wearing a hat, of medium height, wearing a black coat and skirt, with a white shirt. She carried a large bag with a plaid pattern, had a couple of jingling bracelets on her wrists, and wore several rings on her fingers. Overall, based on his experience, he judged her to be a good woman. Not picky, easy to get along with, generous, and kind-hearted. Reliable? He couldn't rely on it, because he couldn't rely on it.

[2910] "Perjury? Me?"
[2911] "Yes. It's a serious offense, you know. You might get into a dispute, even go to jail. [2912] Mrs. Rivard stood up. She straightened her body, and her eyes shone. In that moment, she looked almost dignified.

If he was a notorious con artist, we would have to find those he may have cheated; if he was a detective, we would look for those who had committed a crime under his nose; if he was a rich man, we would look among his heirs. But if we don't know who the man was, finding a suspect from this vast pool is a very daunting task."
[3106] "Without considering Miss Pemukh and Miss Hila Webber, who should not have been there, the answer is disappointing. I think only Mr. Lymes has something strange?" Poirot looked at me inquiringly, and I nodded. "Everyone has a reason to be trusted. Brand is a famous local architect, MacNorton is a professor at Cambridge University, Mrs. Hamm is the widow of a local auctioneer, and the Waterhouse siblings are respected locals. So let's go back to Mr. Curry. Where does he come from? What brought him to 19 Wilbraham Crescent? The neighbor, Mrs. Hamm, said something very revealing when she found out that the dead man was not living at number 19. 'Oh! I see. He came here to die. How strange.' She has a talent for seeing the essence of things, a talent that only those who are only concerned with their own actions and do not care about others possess. She summed up the whole case. Mr. Curry came to 19 Wilbraham Crescent to die. That's all there is to it!"
[3107] "Her words suddenly startled me," I said.
[3108] Poirot did not notice me.
[3109] " 'Dear, dear, dear, come to die.' Mr. Curry came and was killed. But that's not all. His identity cannot be confirmed, which is important. He had no wallet, no identification, and the brand name on his clothes had been torn off. But that's not enough. The card of an insurance agent named Curry was just a temporary measure. If the man's identity cannot be confirmed, he will eventually be given a false identity. It could be his brother, his sister, or his wife. As for the wife, Mrs. Rivale, just the name may have already aroused suspicion. There is a village called Curry-Rivale in Somerset, where I and a friend live nearby – I don't know what the two names suggest, Mr. Curry, Mrs. Rivale."
[3110] "At this point, the plan is almost clear, but what confuses me is why our killer assumes that the police will never be able to determine the actual identity of the deceased. If the man had no family, at least there would be a butler, a servant, or a business partner. This leads me to another assumption: no one knows that the man has disappeared. Another assumption is that he is not British, but only a traveler in this country. This fits with the fact that he had traces of having his teeth treated, but there was no record of any treatment."
[3111] "My mind began to form a vague picture of the victim and the killer. The case was carefully planned and skillfully executed, but now there is a flaw that the killer did not anticipate."
[3112] "What?" Hercule asked.
[3113] To my surprise, at this point Poirot tilted his head back dramatically and recited a poem:
[3114] A nail lost, a shoe lost,
[3115] A shoe lost, a horse lost,
[3116] One war horse was lost, one war was lost,
[3117] One war was lost, one empire was lost,
[3118] All because one horseshoe nail was missing.
[3119] He leaned forward.
[3120] "There may be many suspects for the murder of Mr. Curry. But there is only one who could have killed, or had a reason to kill, the girl Iona."
[3121] We all turned to him at the same time.
[3122] "Let's consider Cavenish Typewriting Agency. There are eight girls working there. On September 9th, four of them were out on an errand, so they wouldn't be back until after lunch. Normally, they would have lunch between 12:30 and 1:30 p.m. The other four, Hilary Webb, Ingrid Brent, and two others, would have lunch between 1:30 and 2:30 p.m. But on that day, Ingrid Brent had a small accident on her way back to the office. Her shoe heel got stuck in the grating, making it difficult for her to walk. So she bought some bread and went straight back to the office."
[3123] Poirot wagged his strong finger at us.
[3124] "We know that Ingrid Brent was worried about something. She tried to meet Hilary Webb outside the office, but failed. Could this be related to Hilary Webb? We couldn't find any evidence. She might have just wanted to ask Hilary Webb about something that was bothering her. But there was one thing that was clear. She wanted to talk to Hilary Webb outside the office."
[3125] "Her statement to the police, that morning, was the only clue we had to what was bothering her. She said, 'I don't know if what she said is true.' Three women testified that day. She might have been referring to Miss Martin."

Hill went to see Miss Martindeer, who was told there was an appointment to go out. The nature and time of the appointment, Ina did not know.

The more Ina thought about it, the more puzzled she became. She had to ask Hill. Hill might know.
[3133] "Then the interrogation began. All the girls attended. Miss Martindeer repeated her statement about the call, but Ina knew that the clear evidence and precise time she provided were false. [3135] "Yes, that's right. Maybe Miss Martindeer was talking to her, and they were walking down the street together when Ina casually expressed her doubts. Tell them we are going to see them right now.' Following someone's instructions was a habit Ina had formed. She went inside, picked up the phone, and Miss Martindeer followed her, standing behind her, tightening her scarf around her neck, and choking her to death."
[3136] "Did nobody see?"
[3137] Poirot shrugged.
[3138] "It should have been someone who saw! But it was exactly 1:00. Lunchtime. And people on New Moon Street were busy looking at this and that. This gave the bold and shameless person the opportunity to act."
[3139] Hercule Poirot shook his head, incredulous.
[3140] "Miss Martin? I don't understand how she got involved in this case?"
[3141] "Indeed, it was not immediately apparent. But there is no doubt that Miss Marple killed Ingrid. Oh, yes. Only she could have killed Ingrid, so she must be involved. From Miss Marple, I began to suspect that this murder was a Macbethian[1] one, a cruel and unimaginative boring woman.”

She inherited a large sum of money from her uncle in Canada, who was her only surviving relative in the family."
[3147] Hercule Poirot sat up alertly.
[3148] "So you think –"
[3149] Poirot leaned back in his chair, folding his fingers together. He half-closed his eyes, as if dreaming.
[3150] "For example, you are a man, a very ordinary, cautious man, with financial difficulties. One day, you receive a letter from a law firm saying that your wife has inherited a large sum of money from her uncle in Canada. Imagine how sad and frustrating that would be! But suddenly, there's a solution. Who would know that this was not Mrs. Brand? In Crofton, no one knew that Mr. Brand had been married before. His first marriage was many years ago, during the war, when he was overseas. His wife died soon after they married, and he remarried quickly. He had the original marriage certificate, various other documents, and photos of all the relatives in Canada. Everything was in order. Anyway, it was worth taking a risk. They tried, and succeeded. They went through all the legal procedures. The Brand couple became rich, and their financial difficulties ended –"
[3151] "However, a year later, something unexpected happened to them. What happened? Mrs. Brand could pretend to be sick, or she could go abroad, but any similar thing would arouse suspicion. The visitor would insist on seeing the woman he had come to visit specially."

If I were the killer, I would go to France or Belgium for a carefree day of sightseeing, and then deliberately throw the dead man's passport in a train or a car, so that the interrogation could take place there."

[3168] Hercule suddenly exclaimed loudly:

[3170] "But these were not her plans. That's what makes it interesting. Everything was here, waiting for her. [3171] "But these clocks must have meant something – in Mr. Greggson's story, I think."

[3173] Hercule didn't want to hear any more about the past. He put away the notebook and took the hotel business card from my hand. Staring at it, I realized how foolish I had been.

[3256] A letter from Inspector Hercule Poirot to Hercule Poirot:
[3257] Dear M. Poirot,
[3258] We have found evidence that you may be interested in hearing about. About four weeks ago, a Mr. Quentin Dugastlin left Canada for Europe. He has no relatives and the date of his return is uncertain. His passport was found by the owner of a small restaurant in Bremen and turned over to the police. It has not been claimed yet.
[3259] Mr. Dugastlin was an old friend of the Montreux family of Quebec. Mr. Henry Montreux, the head of the family, passed away eighteen months ago, leaving a considerable amount of property to his only surviving relative, his niece, Valerie, the wife of Mr. Jocelyn Brand of Portbury, England. A well-known London law firm is handling the Canadian executors. Because the family did not approve of her marriage, Mrs. Brand and the entire family have been cut off from contact with her since her marriage.
[3260] Mr. Dugastlin mentioned to a friend that he planned to visit the Brand family when he went to London, as he has always been fond of Valerie.
[3261] The body that was thought to be Harry Castleton was actually identified as Quentin Durgeslin. The wooden coffin in which the body was found was later discovered hidden in a corner of the Brand family's backyard. Although paint had been applied to the outside of the coffin to cover it up, experts were able to uncover the words "Snowflake Laundry" underneath.
[3262] I will not go into further details to avoid causing you unnecessary worry, but the prosecutor believes that an arrest warrant can be issued for Mrs. Brand.
[3263] Miss Martinchel and Mrs. Brand, as you suspected, are sisters. Although I agree with your idea that she was involved in the crime, obtaining sufficient evidence is proving to be difficult. She is undoubtedly a very intelligent woman. However, I still hold out hope for Mrs. Brand. She is the type to easily switch allegiances.
[3264] Mrs. Brand's first husband died during the war in France, and her second husband, named Hilary Martinchel (who served in the British Army Medical Corps), was also married to her in France. It is clear that they were married there, although detailed records were destroyed at the time.
[3265] It was a pleasure meeting you that day, and I must thank you for the very useful advice you provided. I hope your renovation of your London residence is going smoothly.
[3266] Your sincere friend,
